id="cite_ref-sse_9-11"><a href="#cite_note-sse-9">[9]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#FCC857"><a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Helium">Helium</a></th><td style="padding:2px">24.85%</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding:2px"><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">Oxygen</a></th><td style="padding:2px">0.77%</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding:2px"><a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">Carbon</a></th><td style="padding:2px">0.29%</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding:2px"><a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">Iron</a></th><td style="padding:2px">0.16%</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding:2px"><a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">Neon</a></th><td style="padding:2px">0.12%</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding:2px"><a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">Nitrogen</a></th><td style="padding:2px">0.09%</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding:2px"><a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">Silicon</a></th><td style="padding:2px">0.07%</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding:2px"><a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">Magnesium</a></th><td style="padding:2px">0.05%</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding:2px"><a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">Sulfur</a></th><td style="padding:2px">0.04%</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Sun</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> at the center of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. It is a nearly perfect sphere of hot <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> with internal <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convective</a> motion that generates a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> via a <a href="/wiki/Solar_dynamo" title="Solar dynamo">dynamo process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.1146/annurev-astro-081913-040012_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1146/annurev-astro-081913-040012-17">[17]</a></sup> It is by far the most important source of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. Its diameter is about 1.39 million kilometers (864,000 miles), or 109 times that of Earth, and <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">its mass</a> is about 330,000 times that of Earth. It accounts for about 99.86% of the total mass of the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woolfson00_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woolfson00-18">[18]</a></sup>\nRoughly three quarters of the Sun\'s mass consists of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> (~73%); the rest is mostly <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> (~25%), with much smaller quantities of heavier elements, including <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basu2008_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-basu2008-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Sun is a <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">G-type main-sequence star</a> (G2V) based on its <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">spectral class</a>. As such, it is informally and not completely accurately referred to as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yellow_dwarf" title="Yellow dwarf">yellow dwarf</a> (its light is closer to white than yellow). It formed approximately 4.6 billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-short_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-short-20">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonanno_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bonanno-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Connelly2012_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Connelly2012-21">[20]</a></sup> years ago from the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_collapse" title="Gravitational collapse">gravitational collapse</a> of matter within a region of a large <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a>. Most of this matter gathered in the center, whereas the rest flattened into an orbiting disk that <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">became the Solar System</a>. The central mass became so hot and dense that it eventually initiated <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> in its <a href="/wiki/Solar_core" title="Solar core">core</a>. It is thought that almost all stars <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">form by this process</a>.\n</p><p>The Sun is roughly middle-aged; it has not changed dramatically for more than four billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-short_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-short-20">[a]</a></sup> years, and will remain fairly stable for more than another five billion years. It currently <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fuses</a> about 600 million tons of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> into <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> every second, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass-energy_equivalence" title="Mass-energy equivalence">converting 4 million tons of matter into energy</a> every second as a result. This energy, which can take between 10,000 and 170,000 years to escape from its core, is the source of the Sun\'s light and heat. In about 5 billion years, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fusion" title="Hydrogen fusion">hydrogen fusion</a> in its core has diminished to the point at which the Sun is no longer in <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>, its core will undergo a marked increase in density and temperature while its outer layers expand to eventually become a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a>. It is calculated that the Sun will become sufficiently large to engulf the current orbits of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, and render <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> uninhabitable. After this, it will shed its outer layers and become a dense type of cooling star known as a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a>, and no longer produce energy by fusion, but still glow and give off heat from its previous fusion.\n</p><p>The enormous effect of the Sun on Earth has been recognized since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_times" title="Prehistoric times">prehistoric times</a>, and the Sun has been <a href="/wiki/The_Sun_in_culture" title="The Sun in culture">regarded by some cultures</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Solar_deity" title="Solar deity">deity</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Synodic_day" title="Synodic day">synodic</a> rotation of Earth and its orbit around the Sun are the basis of <a href="/wiki/Solar_calendar" title="Solar calendar">solar calendars</a>, one of which is the predominant <a href="/wiki/Calendar" title="Calendar">calendar</a> in use today.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_etymology">Name and etymology</span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> proper name <i>Sun</i> developed from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>sunne</i> and may be related to <i>south</i>. Cognates to English <i>sun</i> appear in other <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a> <i>sunne</i>, <i>sonne</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">Old Saxon</a> <i>sunna</i>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Dutch" title="Middle Dutch">Middle Dutch</a> <i>sonne</i>, modern <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <i>zon</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>sunna</i>, modern German <i>Sonne</i>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>sunna</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a> <i>sunn&#333;</i>. All Germanic terms for the Sun stem from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> *<i>sunn&#333;n</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BARNHART776_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-BARNHART776-22">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MALLORY129_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-MALLORY129-23">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> name for the Sun, <i>Sol</i>, is used in science fiction as another name for the Sun, but is not commonly used in everyday English. <i>Sol</i> is also used by planetary astronomers to refer to the duration of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_day" title="Solar day">solar day</a> on another planet, such as <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The related word <i>solar</i> is the usual <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectival</a> term used for the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> in terms such as solar day, <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. A mean Earth solar day is approximately 24 hours, whereas a mean Martian \'sol\' is 24 hours, 39 minutes, and 35.244 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The English weekday name <i><a href="/wiki/Sunday" title="Sunday">Sunday</a></i> stems from Old English (<i>Sunnand&#230;g</i>; "Sun\'s day", from before 700) and is ultimately a result of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interpretatio_germanica" title="Interpretatio germanica">Germanic interpretation</a> of Latin <i>dies solis</i>, itself a translation of the Greek &#7969;&#956;&#941;&#961;&#945; &#7969;&#955;&#943;&#959;&#965; (<i>h&#275;m&#233;ra h&#275;l&#237;ou</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BARNHART778_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-BARNHART778-28">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_characteristics">General characteristics</span></h2>\n<p>The Sun is a <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">G-type main-sequence star</a> that comprises about 99.86% of the mass of the Solar System. The Sun has an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> of +4.83, estimated to be brighter than about 85% of the stars in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, most of which are <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>\nThe Sun is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_I_stars" title="Population I stars">Population I</a>, or heavy-element-rich,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heavy_elements_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-heavy_elements-31">[b]</a></sup> star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeilik_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-zeilik-32">[30]</a></sup> The formation of the Sun may have been triggered by shockwaves from one or more nearby <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falk_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Falk-33">[31]</a></sup> This is suggested by a high <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">abundance</a> of heavy elements in the Solar System, such as <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a>, relative to the abundances of these elements in so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_II" title="Population II">Population II</a>, heavy-element-poor, stars. The heavy elements could most plausibly have been produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a> nuclear reactions during a supernova, or by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmutation</a> through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_absorption" title="Neutron absorption">neutron absorption</a> within a massive second-generation star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zeilik_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-zeilik-32">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Sun is by far the brightest object in the Earth\'s sky, with an <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> of &#8722;26.74.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> This is about 13 billion times brighter than the next brightest star, <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a>, which has an apparent magnitude of &#8722;1.46. The mean distance of the Sun\'s center to Earth\'s center is approximately 1 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a> (about 150,000,000&#160;km; 93,000,000&#160;mi), though the distance varies as Earth moves from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a> in January to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a> in July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USNO_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-USNO-36">[34]</a></sup> At this average distance, light travels from the Sun\'s horizon to Earth\'s horizon in about 8 minutes and 19 seconds, while light from the closest points of the Sun and Earth takes about two seconds less. The energy of this <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> supports almost all life<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[c]</a></sup> on Earth by <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon2001_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simon2001-38">[35]</a></sup> and drives <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_climate" title="Earth\'s climate">Earth\'s climate</a> and weather.\n</p><p>The Sun does not have a definite boundary, but its density decreases exponentially with increasing height above the <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">photosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beer_et_al,_2012-41_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beer_et_al,_2012-41-39">[36]</a></sup> For the purpose of measurement, however, the Sun\'s radius is considered to be the distance from its center to the edge of the photosphere, the apparent visible surface of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips1995-73_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips1995-73-40">[37]</a></sup> By this measure, the Sun is a near-perfect sphere with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblateness" title="Oblateness">oblateness</a> estimated at about 9 millionths,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Godier_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Godier-41">[38]</a></sup> which means that its polar diameter differs from its equatorial diameter by only 10 kilometres (6.2&#160;mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfect_sphere_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-perfect_sphere-42">[39]</a></sup>\nThe tidal effect of the planets is weak and does not significantly affect the shape of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schutz2003_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schutz2003-43">[40]</a></sup> The Sun rotates faster at its <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> than at its <a href="/wiki/Poles_of_astronomical_bodies" title="Poles of astronomical bodies">poles</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Solar_rotation" title="Solar rotation">differential rotation</a> is caused by <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convective motion</a> due to heat transport and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis force</a> due to the Sun\'s rotation. In a frame of reference defined by the stars, the rotational period is approximately 25.6 days at the equator and 33.5 days at the poles. Viewed from Earth as it orbits the Sun, the <i>apparent rotational period</i> of the Sun at its equator is about 28 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips1995-78_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips1995-78-44">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunlight">Sunlight</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">Sunlight</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_constant" title="Solar constant">solar constant</a> is the amount of power that the Sun deposits per unit area that is directly exposed to sunlight. The solar constant is equal to approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003136800000000000&#9824;</span>1,368&#160;W/m<sup>2</sup></span> (watts per square meter) at a distance of one <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical unit</a> (AU) from the Sun (that is, on or near Earth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TSI_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-TSI-45">[42]</a></sup> Sunlight on the surface of Earth is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attenuation_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Attenuation (electromagnetic radiation)">attenuated</a> by <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth\'s atmosphere</a>, so that less power arrives at the surface (closer to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003100000000000000&#9824;</span>1,000&#160;W/m<sup>2</sup></span>) in clear conditions when the Sun is near the <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-El-Sharkawi2005_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-El-Sharkawi2005-46">[43]</a></sup> Sunlight at the top of Earth\'s atmosphere is composed (by total energy) of about 50% infrared light, 40% visible light, and 10% ultraviolet light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solar_radiation_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solar_radiation-47">[44]</a></sup> The atmosphere in particular filters out over 70% of solar ultraviolet, especially at the shorter wavelengths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup> Solar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a> ionizes Earth\'s dayside upper atmosphere, creating the electrically conducting <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips1995_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips1995-49">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Sun\'s color is white, with a <a href="/wiki/CIE_1931_color_space" title="CIE 1931 color space">CIE</a> color-space index near (0.3, 0.3), when viewed from space or when the Sun is high in the sky. When measuring all the photons emitted, the Sun is actually emitting more photons in the green portion of the spectrum than any other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> When the Sun is low in the sky, <a href="/wiki/Diffuse_sky_radiation" title="Diffuse sky radiation">atmospheric scattering</a> renders the Sun yellow, red, orange, or magenta. Despite its typical whiteness, most people mentally picture the Sun as yellow; the reasons for this are the subject of debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yellow_sun_paradox_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-yellow_sun_paradox-52">[49]</a></sup>\nThe Sun is a <a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">G2V</a> star, with <i>G2</i> indicating its <a href="/wiki/Effective_temperature" title="Effective temperature">surface temperature</a> of approximately 5,778&#160;K (5,505&#160;&#176;C, 9,941&#160;&#176;F), and <i>V</i> that it, like most stars, is a <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence</a> star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips1995-47_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips1995-47-53">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup> The average <a href="/wiki/Luminance" title="Luminance">luminance</a> of the Sun is about 1.88&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giga" title="Giga">giga</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Candela_per_square_metre" title="Candela per square metre">candela per square metre</a>, but as viewed through Earth\'s atmosphere, this is lowered to about 1.44&#160;Gcd/m<sup>2</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[d]</a></sup> However, the luminance is not constant across the disk of the Sun (<a href="/wiki/Limb_darkening" title="Limb darkening">limb darkening</a>).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BLUE_STEREO_3D_Time_for_Space_Wiggle.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="891" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/BLUE_STEREO_3D_Time_for_Space_Wiggle.gif/220px-BLUE_STEREO_3D_Time_for_Space_Wiggle.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/BLUE_STEREO_3D_Time_for_Space_Wiggle.gif/330px-BLUE_STEREO_3D_Time_for_Space_Wiggle.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/BLUE_STEREO_3D_Time_for_Space_Wiggle.gif/440px-BLUE_STEREO_3D_Time_for_Space_Wiggle.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BLUE_STEREO_3D_Time_for_Space_Wiggle.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>False-color wiggle animation of the Sun by <a href="/wiki/STEREO" title="STEREO">STEREO</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Molecules_in_stars" title="Molecules in stars">Molecules in stars</a></div>\n<p>The Sun is composed primarily of the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>. At this time in the Sun\'s life, they account for 74.9% and 23.8% of the mass of the Sun in the photosphere, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lodders_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-lodders-56">[52]</a></sup> All heavier elements, called <i><a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metals</a></i> in astronomy, account for less than 2% of the mass, with oxygen (roughly 1% of the Sun\'s mass), carbon (0.3%), neon (0.2%), and iron (0.2%) being the most abundant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hkt2004_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-hkt2004-57">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Sun\'s original chemical composition was inherited from the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> out of which it formed. Originally it would have contained about 71.1% hydrogen, 27.4% helium, and 1.5% heavier elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lodders_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-lodders-56">[52]</a></sup>  The hydrogen and most of the helium in the Sun would have been produced by <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a> in the first 20 minutes of the universe, and the heavier elements were <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">produced by previous generations of stars</a> before the Sun was formed, and spread into the interstellar medium during the <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">final stages of stellar life</a> and by events such as <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hkt2004_78_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-hkt2004_78-58">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the Sun formed, the main fusion process has involved fusing hydrogen into helium. Over the past 4.6 billion years, the amount of helium and its location within the Sun has gradually changed. Within the core, the proportion of helium has increased from about 24% to about 60% due to fusion, and some of the helium and heavy elements have settled from the photosphere towards the center of the Sun because of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. The proportions of metals (heavier elements) is unchanged. <a href="/wiki/Heat_transfer" title="Heat transfer">Heat is transferred</a> outward from the Sun\'s core by radiation rather than by convection (see <a href="#Radiative_zone">Radiative zone</a> below), so the fusion products are not lifted outward by heat; they remain in the core<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hkt2004_9.2.3_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-hkt2004_9.2.3-59">[55]</a></sup>  and gradually an inner core of helium has begun to form that cannot be fused because presently the Sun\'s core is not hot or dense enough to fuse helium. In the current photosphere the helium fraction is reduced, and the <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a> is only 84% of what it was in the <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">protostellar</a> phase (before nuclear fusion in the core started). In the future, helium will continue to accumulate in the core, and in about 5 billion years this gradual build-up will eventually cause the Sun to exit the <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a> and become a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The chemical composition of the photosphere is normally considered representative of the composition of the primordial Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aller1968_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-aller1968-61">[57]</a></sup>  The solar heavy-element abundances described above are typically measured both using <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a> of the Sun\'s photosphere and by measuring abundances in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteorites" title="Meteorites">meteorites</a> that have never been heated to melting temperatures. These meteorites are thought to retain the composition of the protostellar Sun and are thus not affected by settling of heavy elements. The two methods generally agree well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-basu2008_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-basu2008-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Singly_ionized_iron-group_elements">Singly ionized iron-group elements</span></h3>\n<p>In the 1970s, much research focused on the abundances of <a href="/wiki/Iron_group" title="Iron group">iron-group</a> elements in the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biemont1978_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-biemont1978-62">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup> Although significant research was done, until 1978 it was difficult to determine the abundances of some iron-group elements (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>) via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectrography" title="Spectrography">spectrography</a> because of their <a href="/wiki/Hyperfine_structure" title="Hyperfine structure">hyperfine structures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biemont1978_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-biemont1978-62">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first largely complete set of <a href="/wiki/Oscillator_strength" title="Oscillator strength">oscillator strengths</a> of singly ionized iron-group elements were made available in the 1960s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[60]</a></sup> and these were subsequently improved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> In 1978, the abundances of singly ionized elements of the iron group were derived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biemont1978_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-biemont1978-62">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopic_composition">Isotopic composition</span></h3>\n<p>Various authors have considered the existence of a gradient in the <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopic</a> compositions of solar and planetary <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gases</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> e.g. correlations between isotopic compositions of <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">xenon</a> in the Sun and on the planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prior to 1983, it was thought that the whole Sun has the same composition as the solar atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manuel1983_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-manuel1983-68">[64]</a></sup> In 1983, it was claimed that it was <a href="/wiki/Fractionation" title="Fractionation">fractionation</a> in the Sun itself that caused the isotopic-composition relationship between the planetary and solar-wind-implanted noble gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manuel1983_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-manuel1983-68">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_fusion">Structure and fusion</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Standard_solar_model" title="Standard solar model">Standard solar model</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sun_poster.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Sun_poster.svg/500px-Sun_poster.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Sun_poster.svg/750px-Sun_poster.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Sun_poster.svg/1000px-Sun_poster.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sun_poster.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The structure of the Sun</div></div></div>\n<p>The structure of the Sun contains the following layers:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><ul><li><b>Core</b> &#8211; the innermost 20-25% of the Sun\'s radius, where temperature (energies) and pressure are sufficient for <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> to occur. Hydrogen fuses into helium (which cannot currently be fused at this point in the Sun\'s life). The fusion process releases energy, and the helium gradually accumulates to form an inner core of helium within the core itself.</li>\n<li><b>Radiative zone</b> &#8211; <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">Convection</a> cannot occur until much nearer the surface of the Sun. Therefore, between about 20-25% of the radius, and 70% of the radius, there is a "radiative zone" in which energy transfer occurs by means of radiation (photons) rather than by convection.</li>\n<li><b>Tachocline</b> &#8211; the boundary region between the radiative and convective zones.</li>\n<li><b>Convective zone</b> &#8211; Between about 70% of the Sun\'s radius and a point close to the visible surface, the Sun is cool and diffuse enough for convection to occur, and this becomes the primary means of outward heat transfer, similar to weather cells which form in the earth\'s atmosphere.</li>\n<li><b>Photosphere</b> &#8211; the deepest part of the Sun which we can directly observe with visible light. Because the Sun is a gaseous object, it does not have a clearly-defined surface; its visible parts are usually divided into a \'photosphere\' and \'atmosphere\'.</li>\n<li><b>Atmosphere</b> &#8211; a gaseous \'halo\' surrounding the Sun, comprising the <a href="/wiki/Chromosphere" title="Chromosphere">chromosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_transition_region" title="Solar transition region">solar transition region</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a>. These can be seen when the main part of the Sun is hidden, for example, during a <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipse</a>.</li></ul></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Core">Core</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_core" title="Solar core">Solar core</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Solar_core" title="Solar core">core</a> of the Sun extends from the center to about 20&#8211;25% of the solar radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garcia2007_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Garcia2007-69">[65]</a></sup> It has a density of up to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005150000000000000&#9824;</span>150&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basu_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basu-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA1_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA1-71">[67]</a></sup> (about 150 times the density of water) and a temperature of close to 15.7 million <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvins</a> (K).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA1_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA1-71">[67]</a></sup> By contrast, the Sun\'s surface temperature is approximately 5,800&#160;K. Recent analysis of <a href="/wiki/Solar_and_Heliospheric_Observatory" title="Solar and Heliospheric Observatory">SOHO</a> mission data favors a faster rotation rate in the core than in the radiative zone above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garcia2007_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Garcia2007-69">[65]</a></sup> Through most of the Sun\'s life, energy has been produced by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> in the core region through a series of steps called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton-proton_chain_reaction" title="Proton-proton chain reaction">p&#8211;p (proton&#8211;proton) chain</a>; this process converts <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> into <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup> Only 0.8% of the energy generated in the Sun comes from the <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a>, though this proportion is expected to increase as the Sun becomes older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpcs271_1_012031_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpcs271_1_012031-73">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The core is the only region in the Sun that produces an appreciable amount of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> through fusion; 99% of the power is generated within 24% of the Sun\'s radius, and by 30% of the radius, fusion has stopped nearly entirely. The remainder of the Sun is heated by this energy as it is transferred outwards through many successive layers, finally to the solar photosphere where it escapes into space as sunlight or the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of particles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips1995-47_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips1995-47-53">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zirker2002-15_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zirker2002-15-74">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton%E2%80%93proton_chain" title="Proton&#8211;proton chain">proton&#8211;proton chain</a> occurs around <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7037919999999999999&#9824;</span>9.2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>37</sup></span> times each second in the core, converting about 3.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">38</span></sup> protons into <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> (helium nuclei) every second (out of a total of ~8.9<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">56</span></sup> free protons in the Sun), or about 6.2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">11</span></sup> kg/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips1995-47_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Phillips1995-47-53">[50]</a></sup> Fusing four free <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> (hydrogen nuclei) into a single <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a> (helium nucleus) releases around 0.7% of the fused mass as energy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup> so the Sun releases energy at the mass&#8211;energy conversion rate of 4.26 million metric tons per second (which requires 600 metric megatons of hydrogen <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup>), for 384.6&#160;<a href="/wiki/Yotta-" title="Yotta-">yottawatts</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7026384600000000000&#9824;</span>3.846<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#215;</span>10<sup>26</sup>&#160;W</span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc_2-16"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc-2">[2]</a></sup> or 9.192<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">10</span></sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/TNT_equivalent" title="TNT equivalent">megatons</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinitrotoluene" title="Trinitrotoluene">TNT</a> per second. However, the large power output of the Sun is mainly due to the huge size and density of its core (compared to Earth and objects on Earth), with only a fairly small amount of power being generated per <a href="/wiki/Cubic_metre" title="Cubic metre">cubic metre</a>. Theoretical models of the Sun\'s interior indicate a power density, or energy production, of approximately 276.5 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> per cubic metre,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup> which is about the same rate of power production as takes place in <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptile</a> <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compost_pile" title="Compost pile">compost pile</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-power_production_density_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-power_production_density-79">[e]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The fusion rate in the core is in a self-correcting equilibrium: a slightly higher rate of fusion would cause the core to heat up more and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">expand</a> slightly against the weight of the outer layers, reducing the density and hence the fusion rate and correcting the <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbation</a>; and a slightly lower rate would cause the core to cool and shrink slightly, increasing the density and increasing the fusion rate and again reverting it to its present rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[76]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiative_zone">Radiative zone</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiative_zone" title="Radiative zone">Radiative zone</a></div>\n<p>From the core out to about 0.7 solar radii, <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal radiation</a> is the primary means of energy transfer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-82">[77]</a></sup> The temperature drops from approximately 7 million to 2 million kelvins with increasing distance from the core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA1_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-NASA1-71">[67]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Temperature_gradient" title="Temperature gradient">temperature gradient</a> is less than the value of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic_lapse_rate" title="Adiabatic lapse rate">adiabatic lapse rate</a> and hence cannot drive convection, which explains why the transfer of energy through this zone is by <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a> instead of thermal <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA1_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-NASA1-71">[67]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ions" title="Ions">Ions</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> emit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photons" title="Photons">photons</a>, which travel only a brief distance before being reabsorbed by other ions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-82">[77]</a></sup> The density drops a hundredfold (from 20 g/cm<sup>3</sup> to 0.2 g/cm<sup>3</sup>) from 0.25 solar radii to the 0.7 radii, the top of the radiative zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-82">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tachocline">Tachocline</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tachocline" title="Tachocline">Tachocline</a></div>\n<p>The radiative zone and the convective zone are separated by a transition layer, the <a href="/wiki/Tachocline" title="Tachocline">tachocline</a>. This is a region where the sharp regime change between the uniform rotation of the radiative zone and the differential rotation of the convection zone results in a large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shear_(fluid)" title="Shear (fluid)">shear</a> between the two&#8212;a condition where successive horizontal layers slide past one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup> Presently, it is hypothesized (see <a href="/wiki/Solar_dynamo" title="Solar dynamo">Solar dynamo</a>) that a magnetic dynamo within this layer generates the Sun\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA1_71-4"><a href="#cite_note-NASA1-71">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Convective_zone">Convective zone</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Convection_zone" title="Convection zone">Convection zone</a></div>\n<p>The Sun\'s convection zone extends from 0.7 solar radii (500,000&#160;km) to near the surface. In this layer, the solar plasma is not dense enough or hot enough to transfer the heat energy of the interior outward via radiation. Instead, the density of the plasma is low enough to allow convective currents to develop and move the Sun\'s energy outward towards its surface. Material heated at the tachocline picks up heat and expands, thereby reducing its density and allowing it to rise. As a result, an orderly motion of the mass develops into <a href="/wiki/Thermal" title="Thermal">thermal cells</a> that carry the majority of the heat outward to the Sun\'s photosphere above. Once the material diffusively and radiatively cools just beneath the photospheric surface, its density increases, and it sinks to the base of the convection zone, where it again picks up heat from the top of the radiative zone and the convective cycle continues. At the photosphere, the temperature has dropped to 5,700 K and the density to only 0.2 g/m<sup>3</sup> (about 1/6,000 the density of air at sea level).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA1_71-5"><a href="#cite_note-NASA1-71">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The thermal columns of the convection zone form an imprint on the surface of the Sun giving it a granular appearance  called the <a href="/wiki/Granule_(solar_physics)" title="Granule (solar physics)">solar granulation</a> at the smallest scale and <a href="/wiki/Supergranulation" title="Supergranulation">supergranulation</a> at larger scales. Turbulent convection in this outer part of the solar interior sustains "small-scale" dynamo action over the near-surface volume of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA1_71-6"><a href="#cite_note-NASA1-71">[67]</a></sup> The Sun\'s thermal columns are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B%C3%A9nard_cells" title="B&#233;nard cells">B&#233;nard cells</a> and take the shape of hexagonal prisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photosphere">Photosphere</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EffectiveTemperature_300dpi_e.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2200" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/EffectiveTemperature_300dpi_e.png/220px-EffectiveTemperature_300dpi_e.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/EffectiveTemperature_300dpi_e.png/330px-EffectiveTemperature_300dpi_e.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/EffectiveTemperature_300dpi_e.png/440px-EffectiveTemperature_300dpi_e.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EffectiveTemperature_300dpi_e.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Effective_temperature" title="Effective temperature">effective temperature</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black body</a> temperature, of the Sun (5,777 K) is the temperature a black body of the same size must have to yield the same total emissive power.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">Photosphere</a></div>\n<p>The visible surface of the Sun, the photosphere, is the layer below which the Sun becomes <a href="/wiki/Opacity_(optics)" title="Opacity (optics)">opaque</a> to visible light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abhyankar1977_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85">[80]</a></sup> Above the photosphere visible sunlight is free to propagate into space, and almost all of its energy escapes the Sun entirely. The change in opacity is due to the decreasing amount of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_anion" title="Hydrogen anion">H<sup>&#8722;</sup> ions</a>, which absorb visible light easily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abhyankar1977_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85">[80]</a></sup> Conversely, the visible light we see is produced as electrons react with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> atoms to produce H<sup>&#8722;</sup> ions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibson_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibson-86">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shu_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shu-87">[82]</a></sup>\nThe photosphere is tens to hundreds of kilometers thick, and is slightly less opaque than air on Earth. Because the upper part of the photosphere is cooler than the lower part, an image of the Sun appears brighter in the center than on the edge or <i>limb</i> of the solar disk, in a phenomenon known as <a href="/wiki/Limb_darkening" title="Limb darkening">limb darkening</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abhyankar1977_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85">[80]</a></sup> The spectrum of sunlight has approximately the spectrum of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black-body" title="Black-body">black-body</a> radiating at 5,777 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>, interspersed with atomic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_line" title="Absorption line">absorption lines</a> from the tenuous layers above the photosphere. The photosphere has a particle density of ~10<sup>23</sup>&#160;m<sup>&#8722;3</sup> (about 0.37% of the particle number per volume of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth\'s atmosphere">Earth\'s atmosphere</a> at sea level). The photosphere is not fully ionized&#8212;the extent of ionization is about 3%, leaving almost all of the hydrogen in atomic form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During early studies of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_spectrum" title="Optical spectrum">optical spectrum</a> of the photosphere, some absorption lines were found that did not correspond to any <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> then known on Earth. In 1868, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Lockyer" title="Norman Lockyer">Norman Lockyer</a> hypothesized that these absorption lines were caused by a new element that he dubbed <i><a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a></i>, after the Greek Sun god <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a>. Twenty-five years later, helium was isolated on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lockyer_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lockyer-89">[84]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">Corona</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coronal_loop" title="Coronal loop">Coronal loop</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2017_Solar_Eclipse_Weiser_Idaho.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/2017_Solar_Eclipse_Weiser_Idaho.jpg/220px-2017_Solar_Eclipse_Weiser_Idaho.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/2017_Solar_Eclipse_Weiser_Idaho.jpg/330px-2017_Solar_Eclipse_Weiser_Idaho.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/2017_Solar_Eclipse_Weiser_Idaho.jpg/440px-2017_Solar_Eclipse_Weiser_Idaho.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2017_Solar_Eclipse_Weiser_Idaho.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>During a total <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipse</a>, the solar <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a> can be seen with the naked eye, during the brief period of totality.</div></div></div>\n<p>During a total <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipse</a>, when the disk of the Sun is covered by that of the Moon, parts of the Sun\'s surrounding atmosphere can be seen. It is composed of four distinct parts: the <a href="/wiki/Chromosphere" title="Chromosphere">chromosphere</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_transition_region" title="Solar transition region">transition region</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a>.\n</p><p>The coolest layer of the Sun is a temperature minimum region extending to about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005500000000000000&#9824;</span>500&#160;km</span> above the photosphere, and has a temperature of about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003410000000000000&#9824;</span>4,100&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abhyankar1977_85-3"><a href="#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85">[80]</a></sup> This part of the Sun is cool enough to allow the existence of simple molecules such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and water, which can be detected via their absorption spectra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solanki1994_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solanki1994-90">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The chromosphere, transition region, and corona are much hotter than the surface of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abhyankar1977_85-4"><a href="#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85">[80]</a></sup> The reason is not well understood, but evidence suggests that <a href="/wiki/Alfv%C3%A9n_wave" title="Alfv&#233;n wave">Alfv&#233;n waves</a> may have enough energy to heat the corona.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Above the temperature minimum layer is a layer about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006200000000000000&#9824;</span>2,000&#160;km</span> thick, dominated by a spectrum of emission and absorption lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abhyankar1977_85-5"><a href="#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85">[80]</a></sup> It is called the <i>chromosphere</i> from the Greek root <i>chroma</i>, meaning color, because the chromosphere is visible as a colored flash at the beginning and end of total <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-82">[77]</a></sup> The temperature of the chromosphere increases gradually with altitude, ranging up to around <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7004200000000000000&#9824;</span>20,000&#160;K</span> near the top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abhyankar1977_85-6"><a href="#cite_note-Abhyankar1977-85">[80]</a></sup> In the upper part of the chromosphere <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> becomes partially <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionized</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansteen1997_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hansteen1997-92">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="712" data-file-width="1014" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg/350px-171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg/525px-171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg/700px-171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:171879main_LimbFlareJan12_lg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Taken by <a href="/wiki/Hinode_(satellite)" title="Hinode (satellite)">Hinode</a>\'s Solar Optical Telescope on 12 January 2007, this image of the Sun reveals the filamentary nature of the plasma connecting regions of different magnetic polarity.</div></div></div>\n<p>Above the chromosphere, in a thin (about 200&#160;km) <a href="/wiki/Solar_transition_region" title="Solar transition region">transition region</a>, the temperature rises rapidly from around 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a> in the upper chromosphere to coronal temperatures closer to 1,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erdelyi2007_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-93">[88]</a></sup> The temperature increase is facilitated by the full ionization of helium in the transition region, which significantly reduces radiative cooling of the plasma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansteen1997_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hansteen1997-92">[87]</a></sup> The transition region does not occur at a well-defined altitude. Rather, it forms a kind of <a href="/wiki/Halo_(optical_phenomenon)" title="Halo (optical phenomenon)">nimbus</a> around chromospheric features such as <a href="/wiki/Spicule_(solar_physics)" title="Spicule (solar physics)">spicules</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_filament" title="Solar filament">filaments</a>, and is in constant, chaotic motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_82-4"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-82">[77]</a></sup> The transition region is not easily visible from Earth\'s surface, but is readily observable from <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a> by instruments sensitive to the <a href="/wiki/Extreme_ultraviolet" title="Extreme ultraviolet">extreme ultraviolet</a> portion of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">spectrum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dwivedi2006_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi2006-94">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">corona</a> is the next layer of the Sun. The low corona, near the surface of the Sun, has a particle density around 10<sup>15</sup>&#160;m<sup>&#8722;3</sup> to 10<sup>16</sup>&#160;m<sup>&#8722;3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansteen1997_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hansteen1997-92">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-particle_density_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-particle_density-95">[f]</a></sup> The average temperature of the corona and solar wind is about 1,000,000&#8211;2,000,000 K; however, in the hottest regions it is 8,000,000&#8211;20,000,000 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erdelyi2007_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-93">[88]</a></sup> Although no complete theory yet exists to account for the temperature of the corona, at least some of its heat is known to be from <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_reconnection" title="Magnetic reconnection">magnetic reconnection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erdelyi2007_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-93">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2001_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2001-96">[90]</a></sup>\nThe corona is the extended atmosphere of the Sun, which has a volume much larger than the volume enclosed by the Sun\'s photosphere. A flow of plasma outward from the Sun into interplanetary space is the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2001_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2001-96">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Heliosphere" title="Heliosphere">heliosphere</a>, the tenuous outermost atmosphere of the Sun, is filled with the solar wind plasma. This outermost layer of the Sun is defined to begin at the distance where the flow of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> becomes <i>superalfv&#233;nic</i>&#8212;that is, where the flow becomes faster than the speed of <a href="/wiki/Alfv%C3%A9n_wave" title="Alfv&#233;n wave">Alfv&#233;n waves</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup> at approximately 20 solar radii (0.1 AU).\nTurbulence and dynamic forces in the heliosphere cannot affect the shape of the solar corona within, because the information can only travel at the speed of Alfv&#233;n waves. The solar wind travels outward continuously through the heliosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup> forming the solar magnetic field into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parker_spiral" title="Parker spiral">spiral</a> shape,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2001_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2001-96">[90]</a></sup> until it impacts the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopause_(astronomy)" title="Heliopause (astronomy)">heliopause</a> more than 50 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> from the Sun. In December 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a> probe passed through a shock front that is thought to be part of the heliopause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup> In late 2012 Voyager 1 recorded a marked increase in <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic ray</a> collisions and a sharp drop in lower energy particles from the solar wind, which suggested that the probe had passed through the heliopause and entered the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photons_and_neutrinos">Photons and neutrinos</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Solar_irradiance" title="Solar irradiance">Solar irradiance</a></div>\n<p>High-energy <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma-ray</a> photons initially released with fusion reactions in the core are almost immediately absorbed by the solar plasma of the radiative zone, usually after traveling only a few millimeters. Re-emission happens in a random direction and usually at a slightly lower energy. With this sequence of emissions and absorptions, it takes a long time for radiation to reach the Sun\'s surface. Estimates of the photon travel time range between 10,000 and 170,000&#160;years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-102">[96]</a></sup> In contrast, it takes only 2.3 seconds for the <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a>, which account for about 2% of the total energy production of the Sun, to reach the surface. Because energy transport in the Sun is a process that involves photons in thermodynamic equilibrium with matter, the time scale of energy transport in the Sun is longer, on the order of 30,000,000 years. This is the time it would take the Sun to return to a stable state, if the rate of energy generation in its core were suddenly changed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neutrinos are also released by the fusion reactions in the core, but, unlike photons, they rarely interact with matter, so almost all are able to escape the Sun immediately. For many years measurements of the number of neutrinos produced in the Sun were <a href="/wiki/Solar_neutrino_problem" title="Solar neutrino problem">lower than theories predicted</a> by a factor of 3. This discrepancy was resolved in 2001 through the discovery of the effects of <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_oscillation" title="Neutrino oscillation">neutrino oscillation</a>: the Sun emits the number of neutrinos predicted by the <a href="/wiki/Theory" title="Theory">theory</a>, but neutrino detectors were missing &#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>2</sup>&#8260;<sub>3</sub></span> of them because the neutrinos had changed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavor_(particle_physics)" title="Flavor (particle physics)">flavor</a> by the time they were detected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlattl_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schlattl-104">[98]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_activity">Magnetic activity</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field">Magnetic field</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Stellar_magnetic_field" title="Stellar magnetic field">Stellar magnetic field</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunspots" title="Sunspots">Sunspots</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_solar_cycles" title="List of solar cycles">List of solar cycles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Solar_phenomena" title="Solar phenomena">Solar phenomena</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/172197main_NASA_Flare_Gband_lg-withouttext.jpg/440px-172197main_NASA_Flare_Gband_lg-withouttext.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div December 2006</div></div></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:354px;max-width:354px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:352px;max-width:352px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" style="margin:1px;width:352px;max-width:352px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunspot_area_variation.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="1613"
