//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Sunspot_area_variation.svg/700px-Sunspot_area_variation.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Solar_cycle#Sunspots" title="Solar cycle">Butterfly diagram</a> showing paired sunspot pattern. Graph is of sunspot area.</div></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sun_-_August_1,_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3647" data-file-width="4095" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sun_-_August_1%2C_2010.jpg/220px-Sun_-_August_1%2C_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sun_-_August_1%2C_2010.jpg/330px-Sun_-_August_1%2C_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sun_-_August_1%2C_2010.jpg/440px-Sun_-_August_1%2C_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sun_-_August_1,_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In this false-color ultraviolet image, the Sun shows a C3-class solar flare (white area on upper left), a solar tsunami (wave-like structure, upper right) and multiple filaments of <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> following a magnetic field, rising from the stellar surface.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="676" data-file-width="866" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif/220px-Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif/330px-Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif/440px-Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Heliospheric_current_sheet" title="Heliospheric current sheet">heliospheric current sheet</a> extends to the outer reaches of the Solar System, and results from the influence of the Sun\'s rotating magnetic field on the <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">plasma</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">interplanetary medium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The Sun has a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> that varies across the surface of the Sun. Its polar field is 1&#8211;2 <a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss</a> (0.0001&#8211;0.0002&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">T</a>), whereas the field is typically 3,000 gauss (0.3&#160;T) in features on the Sun called <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspots</a> and 10&#8211;100 gauss (0.001&#8211;0.01&#160;T) in <a href="/wiki/Solar_prominence" title="Solar prominence">solar prominences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nssdc_2-17"><a href="#cite_note-nssdc-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The magnetic field also varies in time and location. The quasi-periodic 11-year <a href="/wiki/Solar_cycle" title="Solar cycle">solar cycle</a> is the most prominent variation in which the number and size of sunspots waxes and wanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.1146/annurev-astro-081913-040012_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1146/annurev-astro-081913-040012-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zirker2002-119_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zirker2002-119-106">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lang_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lang-107">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sunspots are visible as dark patches on the Sun\'s <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">photosphere</a>, and correspond to concentrations of magnetic field where the <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convective transport</a> of heat is inhibited from the solar interior to the surface. As a result, sunspots are slightly cooler than the surrounding photosphere, and, so, they appear dark. At a typical <a href="/wiki/Solar_minimum" title="Solar minimum">solar minimum</a>, few sunspots are visible, and occasionally none can be seen at all. Those that do appear are at high solar latitudes. As the solar cycle progresses towards its <a href="/wiki/Solar_maximum" title="Solar maximum">maximum</a>, sunspots tend form closer to the solar equator, a phenomenon known as <a href="/wiki/Sp%C3%B6rer%27s_law" title="Sp&#246;rer\'s law">Sp&#246;rer\'s law</a>. The largest sunspots can be tens of thousands of kilometers across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sunspot2001_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sunspot2001-108">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>An 11-year sunspot cycle is half of a 22-year <a href="/wiki/Babcock_Model" title="Babcock Model">Babcock</a>&#8211;Leighton <a href="/wiki/Solar_dynamo" title="Solar dynamo">dynamo</a> cycle, which corresponds to an oscillatory exchange of energy between <a href="/wiki/Toroidal_and_poloidal" title="Toroidal and poloidal">toroidal and poloidal</a> solar magnetic fields. At <a href="/wiki/Solar_maximum" title="Solar maximum">solar-cycle maximum</a>, the external poloidal dipolar magnetic field is near its dynamo-cycle minimum strength, but an internal <a href="/wiki/Toroidal_and_poloidal" title="Toroidal and poloidal">toroidal</a> quadrupolar field, generated through differential rotation within the tachocline, is near its maximum strength. At this point in the dynamo cycle, buoyant upwelling within the convective zone forces emergence of toroidal magnetic field through the photosphere, giving rise to pairs of sunspots, roughly aligned east&#8211;west and having footprints with opposite magnetic polarities. The magnetic polarity of sunspot pairs alternates every solar cycle, a phenomenon known as the Hale cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solarcycle_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-solarcycle-110">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the solar cycle\'s declining phase, energy shifts from the internal toroidal magnetic field to the external poloidal field, and sunspots diminish in number and size. At <a href="/wiki/Solar_minimum" title="Solar minimum">solar-cycle minimum</a>, the toroidal field is, correspondingly, at minimum strength, sunspots are relatively rare, and the poloidal field is at its maximum strength. With the rise of the next 11-year sunspot cycle, differential rotation shifts magnetic energy back from the poloidal to the toroidal field, but with a polarity that is opposite to the previous cycle. The process carries on continuously, and in an idealized, simplified scenario, each 11-year sunspot cycle corresponds to a change, then, in the overall polarity of the Sun\'s large-scale magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The solar magnetic field extends well beyond the Sun itself. The electrically conducting solar wind plasma carries the Sun\'s magnetic field into space, forming what is called the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_magnetic_field" title="Interplanetary magnetic field">interplanetary magnetic field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2001_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2001-96">[90]</a></sup> In an approximation known as ideal <a href="/wiki/Magnetohydrodynamics" title="Magnetohydrodynamics">magnetohydrodynamics</a>, plasma particles only move along the magnetic field lines. As a result, the outward-flowing solar wind stretches the interplanetary magnetic field outward, forcing it into a roughly radial structure. For a simple dipolar solar magnetic field, with opposite hemispherical polarities on either side of the solar magnetic equator, a thin <a href="/wiki/Heliospheric_current_sheet" title="Heliospheric current sheet">current sheet</a> is formed in the solar wind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2001_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2001-96">[90]</a></sup> At great distances, the rotation of the Sun twists the dipolar magnetic field and corresponding current sheet into an <a href="/wiki/Archimedean_spiral" title="Archimedean spiral">Archimedean spiral</a> structure called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parker_spiral" title="Parker spiral">Parker spiral</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2001_96-5"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2001-96">[90]</a></sup> The interplanetary magnetic field is much stronger than the dipole component of the solar magnetic field. The Sun\'s dipole magnetic field of 50&#8211;400&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">&#956;T</a> (at the photosphere) reduces with the inverse-cube of the distance to about 0.1&#160;nT at the distance of Earth. However, according to spacecraft observations the interplanetary field at Earth\'s location is around 5&#160;nT, about a hundred&#160;times greater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang2003_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang2003-113">[107]</a></sup> The difference is due to magnetic fields generated by electrical currents in the plasma surrounding the Sun.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variation_in_activity">Variation in activity</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar-cycle-data.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Solar-cycle-data.png/220px-Solar-cycle-data.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Solar-cycle-data.png/330px-Solar-cycle-data.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Solar-cycle-data.png/440px-Solar-cycle-data.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar-cycle-data.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Measurements from 2005 of solar cycle variation during the last 30 years</div></div></div>\n<p>The Sun\'s magnetic field leads to many effects that are collectively called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_variation" title="Solar variation">solar activity</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_flares" title="Solar flares">Solar flares</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejections" title="Coronal mass ejections">coronal-mass ejections</a> tend to occur at sunspot groups. Slowly changing high-speed streams of <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> are emitted from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronal_holes" title="Coronal holes">coronal holes</a> at the photospheric surface. Both coronal-mass ejections and high-speed streams of solar wind carry plasma and <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_magnetic_field" title="Interplanetary magnetic field">interplanetary magnetic field</a> outward into the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zirker2002_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zirker2002-114">[108]</a></sup> The effects of solar activity on Earth include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">auroras</a> at moderate to high latitudes and the disruption of radio communications and <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electric power</a>. Solar activity is thought to have played a large role in the <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formation and evolution of the Solar System</a>.\n</p><p>With solar-cycle modulation of sunspot number comes a corresponding modulation of <a href="/wiki/Space_weather" title="Space weather">space weather</a> conditions, including those surrounding Earth where technological systems can be affected.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long-term_change">Long-term change</span></h3>\n<p>Long-term secular change in sunspot number is thought, by some scientists, to be correlated with long-term change in solar irradiance,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup> which, in turn, might influence Earth\'s long-term climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup>\nFor example, in the 17th century, the solar cycle appeared to have stopped entirely for several decades; few sunspots were observed during a period known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maunder_minimum" title="Maunder minimum">Maunder minimum</a>. This coincided in time with the era of the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>, when Europe experienced unusually cold temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lean_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lean-117">[111]</a></sup> Earlier extended minima have been discovered through analysis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_ring" title="Tree ring">tree rings</a> and appear to have coincided with lower-than-average global temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[112]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A recent theory claims that there are magnetic instabilities in the core of the Sun that cause fluctuations with periods of either 41,000 or 100,000 years. These could provide a better explanation of the <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice ages</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Milankovitch_cycles" title="Milankovitch cycles">Milankovitch cycles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_phases">Life phases</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Formation and evolution of the Solar System</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">Stellar evolution</a></div>\n<p>The Sun today is roughly halfway through the most stable part of its life. It has not changed dramatically for over four billion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-short_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-short-20">[a]</a></sup> years, and will remain fairly stable for more than five billion more. However, after hydrogen fusion in its core has stopped, the Sun will undergo dramatic changes, both internally and externally.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h3>\n<p>The Sun formed about 4.6 billion years ago from the collapse of part of a giant <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a> that consisted mostly of hydrogen and helium and that probably gave birth to many other stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zirker2002-7_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zirker2002-7-121">[115]</a></sup> This age is estimated using <a href="/wiki/Computer_simulation" title="Computer simulation">computer models</a> of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">stellar evolution</a> and through <a href="/wiki/Nucleocosmochronology" title="Nucleocosmochronology">nucleocosmochronology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonanno_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bonanno-11">[11]</a></sup> The result is consistent with the <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometric date</a> of the oldest Solar System material, at 4.567 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature436_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature436-123">[117]</a></sup> Studies of ancient <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> reveal traces of stable daughter nuclei of short-lived isotopes, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron-60" title="Iron-60">iron-60</a>, that form only in exploding, short-lived stars. This indicates that one or more supernovae must have occurred near the location where the Sun formed. A <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> from a nearby supernova would have triggered the formation of the Sun by compressing the matter within the molecular cloud and causing certain regions to collapse under their own gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[118]</a></sup> As one fragment of the cloud collapsed it also began to rotate because of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum" title="Conservation of angular momentum">conservation of angular momentum</a> and heat up with the increasing pressure. Much of the mass became concentrated in the center, whereas the rest flattened out into a disk that would become the planets and other Solar System bodies. Gravity and pressure within the core of the cloud generated a lot of heat as it accreted more matter from the surrounding disk, eventually triggering <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">nuclear fusion</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/HD_162826" title="HD 162826">HD 162826</a> and <a href="/wiki/HD_186302" title="HD 186302">HD 186302</a> are hypothesized stellar siblings of the Sun, having formed in the same molecular cloud.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_sequence">Main sequence</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_evolution_(English).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="544" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Solar_evolution_%28English%29.svg/320px-Solar_evolution_%28English%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Solar_evolution_%28English%29.svg/480px-Solar_evolution_%28English%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Solar_evolution_%28English%29.svg/640px-Solar_evolution_%28English%29.svg.png 2x" width="320"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_evolution_(English).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Evolution of the Sun\'s <a href="/wiki/Solar_luminosity" title="Solar luminosity">luminosity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_radius" title="Solar radius">radius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Effective_temperature" title="Effective temperature">effective temperature</a> compared to the present Sun. After Ribas (2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ribas2010_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-ribas2010-125">[119]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The Sun is about halfway through its <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence</a> stage, during which nuclear fusion reactions in its core fuse hydrogen into helium. Each second, more than four million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of matter are converted into energy within the Sun\'s core, producing <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar radiation</a>. At this rate, the Sun has so far converted around 100 times the mass of Earth into energy, about 0.03% of the total mass of the Sun. The Sun will spend a total of approximately 10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> years as a main-sequence star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[120]</a></sup> The Sun is gradually becoming hotter during its time on the main sequence, because the helium atoms in the core occupy less volume than the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atoms</a> that were fused. The core is therefore shrinking, allowing the outer layers of the Sun to move closer to the centre and experience a stronger gravitational force, according to the <a href="/wiki/Inverse-square_law" title="Inverse-square law">inverse-square law</a>. This stronger force increases the pressure on the core, which is resisted by a gradual increase in the rate at which fusion occurs. This process speeds up as the core gradually becomes denser. It is estimated that the Sun has become 30% brighter in the last 4.5 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[121]</a></sup> At present, it is increasing in brightness by about 1% every 100 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[122]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_core_hydrogen_exhaustion">After core hydrogen exhaustion</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sun_red_giant.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="646" data-file-width="728" decoding="async" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Sun_red_giant.svg/300px-Sun_red_giant.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Sun_red_giant.svg/450px-Sun_red_giant.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Sun_red_giant.svg/600px-Sun_red_giant.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sun_red_giant.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The size of the current Sun (now in the <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a>) compared to its estimated size during its red-giant phase in the future</div></div></div>\n<p>The Sun does not have enough mass to explode as a <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a>. Instead it will exit the <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a> in approximately 5 billion years and start to turn into a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schroder_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-schroder-130">[124]</a></sup> As a red giant, the Sun will grow so large that it will engulf Mercury, Venus, and probably Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schroder_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-schroder-130">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sackmann_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-sackmann-131">[125]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even before it becomes a red giant, the luminosity of the Sun will have nearly doubled, and Earth will receive as much sunlight as Venus receives today. Once the core hydrogen is exhausted in 5.4 billion years, the Sun will expand into a <a href="/wiki/Subgiant" title="Subgiant">subgiant</a> phase and slowly double in size over about half a billion years. It will then expand more rapidly over about half a billion years until it is over two hundred times larger than today and a couple of thousand times more luminous. This then starts the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_giant_branch" title="Red giant branch">red-giant-branch</a> phase where the Sun will spend around a billion years and lose around a third of its mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schroder_130-2"><a href="#cite_note-schroder-130">[124]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_a_Sun-like_star.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="730" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Evolution_of_a_Sun-like_star.svg/300px-Evolution_of_a_Sun-like_star.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Evolution_of_a_Sun-like_star.svg/450px-Evolution_of_a_Sun-like_star.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Evolution_of_a_Sun-like_star.svg/600px-Evolution_of_a_Sun-like_star.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_a_Sun-like_star.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Evolution of a Sun-like star. The track of a one solar mass star on the <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung&#8211;Russell diagram">Hertzsprung&#8211;Russell diagram</a> is shown from the main sequence to the post-asymptotic-giant-branch stage.</div></div></div>\n<p>After the red-giant branch the Sun has approximately 120 million years of active life left, but much happens. First, the core, full of <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_matter" title="Degenerate matter">degenerate</a> helium ignites violently in the <a href="/wiki/Helium_flash" title="Helium flash">helium flash</a>, where it is estimated that 6% of the core, itself 40% of the Sun\'s mass, will be converted into carbon within a matter of minutes through the <a href="/wiki/Triple-alpha_process" title="Triple-alpha process">triple-alpha process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[126]</a></sup> The Sun then shrinks to around 10 times its current size and 50 times the luminosity, with a temperature a little lower than today. It will then have reached the <a href="/wiki/Red_clump" title="Red clump">red clump</a> or <a href="/wiki/Horizontal_branch" title="Horizontal branch">horizontal branch</a>, but a star of the Sun\'s mass does not evolve blueward along the horizontal branch. Instead, it just becomes moderately larger and more luminous over about 100 million years as it continues to burn helium in the core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schroder_130-3"><a href="#cite_note-schroder-130">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When the helium is exhausted, the Sun will repeat the expansion it followed when the hydrogen in the core was exhausted, except that this time it all happens faster, and the Sun becomes larger and more luminous. This is the <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">asymptotic-giant-branch</a> phase, and the Sun is alternately burning hydrogen in a shell or helium in a deeper shell. After about 20 million years on the early asymptotic giant branch, the Sun becomes increasingly unstable, with rapid mass loss and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_pulse" title="Thermal pulse">thermal pulses</a> that increase the size and luminosity for a few hundred years every 100,000 years or so. The thermal pulses become larger each time, with the later pulses pushing the luminosity to as much as 5,000 times the current level and the radius to over 1 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agb_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-agb-133">[127]</a></sup> According to a 2008 model, Earth\'s orbit is shrinking due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_forces" title="Tidal forces">tidal forces</a> (and, eventually, drag from the lower <a href="/wiki/Chromosphere" title="Chromosphere">chromosphere</a>), so that it will be engulfed by the Sun near the tip of the red giant branch phase, 3.8 and 1 million years after Mercury and Venus have respectively suffered the same fate. Models vary depending on the rate and timing of mass loss. Models that have higher mass loss on the red-giant branch produce smaller, less luminous stars at the tip of the asymptotic giant branch, perhaps only 2,000 times the luminosity and less than 200 times the radius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schroder_130-4"><a href="#cite_note-schroder-130">[124]</a></sup> For the Sun, four thermal pulses are predicted before it completely loses its outer envelope and starts to make a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">planetary nebula</a>. By the end of that phase&#8212;lasting approximately 500,000 years&#8212;the Sun will only have about half of its current mass.\n</p><p>The post-asymptotic-giant-branch evolution is even faster. The luminosity stays approximately constant as the temperature increases, with the ejected half of the Sun\'s mass becoming ionised into a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">planetary nebula</a> as the exposed core reaches 30,000 K. The final naked core, a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a>, will have a temperature of over 100,000 K, and contain an estimated 54.05% of the Sun\'s present day mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schroder_130-5"><a href="#cite_note-schroder-130">[124]</a></sup> The planetary nebula will disperse in about 10,000 years, but the white dwarf will survive for trillions of years before fading to a hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Black_dwarf" title="Black dwarf">black dwarf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloecker1_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloecker1-134">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloecker2_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloecker2-135">[129]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_location">Orbit and location</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_(updated_-_annotated).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5600" data-file-width="5600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg/220px-Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg/330px-Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg/440px-Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_%28updated_-_annotated%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_the_Milky_Way_(updated_-_annotated).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of the Milky Way, showing the location of the Sun</div></div></div>\n<p>The Sun lies close to the inner rim of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Orion_Arm" title="Orion Arm">Orion Arm</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Local_Interstellar_Cloud" title="Local Interstellar Cloud">Local Interstellar Cloud</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Gould_Belt" title="Gould Belt">Gould Belt</a>, at a distance of 7.5&#8211;8.5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiloparsec" title="Kiloparsec">kpc</a> (25,000&#8211;28,000 light-years) from the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">Galactic Center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[131]</a></sup>\n<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-distance1_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-distance1-138">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-distance2_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-distance2-139">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-distance3_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-distance3-140">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eisenhaueretal2005_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-eisenhaueretal2005-141">[135]</a></sup>\nThe Sun is contained within the <a href="/wiki/Local_Bubble" title="Local Bubble">Local Bubble</a>, a space of rarefied hot gas, possibly produced by the supernova remnant <a href="/wiki/Geminga" title="Geminga">Geminga</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[136]</a></sup> or multiple supernovae in subgroup B1 of the Pleiades moving group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[137]</a></sup> The distance between the local arm and the next arm out, the <a href="/wiki/Perseus_Arm" title="Perseus Arm">Perseus Arm</a>, is about 6,500 light-years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn9_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-fn9-144">[138]</a></sup> The Sun, and thus the Solar System, is found in what scientists call the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_habitable_zone" title="Galactic habitable zone">galactic habitable zone</a>.\nThe <i>Apex of the Sun\'s Way</i>, or the <a href="/wiki/Solar_apex" title="Solar apex">solar apex</a>, is the direction that the Sun travels relative to other nearby stars. This motion is towards a point in the constellation <a href="/wiki/Hercules_(constellation)" title="Hercules (constellation)">Hercules</a>, near the star <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">Vega</a>.\n</p><p>Within 32.6&#160;ly of the Sun there are 315 known stars in 227 systems, as of 2000, including 163 single stars. It is estimated that a further 130 systems within this range have not yet been identified. Out to 81.5&#160;ly, there may be up to 7,500 stars, of which around 2,600 are known. The number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substellar_objects" title="Substellar objects">substellar objects</a> in that volume are expected to be comparable to the number of stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scholz2002_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scholz2002-145">[139]</a></sup> Of the 50 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nearest_stars" title="Nearest stars">nearest stellar systems</a> within 17 light-years from Earth (the closest being the red dwarf <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a> at approximately 4.2 light-years), the Sun ranks fourth in mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_in_Milky_Way">Orbit in Milky Way</span></h3>\n<p>The Sun orbits the center of the Milky Way, and it is presently moving in the direction of the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(constellation)" title="Cygnus (constellation)">Cygnus</a>. A simple model of the motion of a star in the galaxy gives the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_coordinates" title="Galactic coordinates">galactic coordinates</a> <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">X</span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">Y</span>, and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">Z</span> as:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle X(t)=X(0)+{\\frac {U(0)}{\\kappa }}\\sin(\\kappa t)+{\\frac {V(0)}{2B}}(1-\\cos(\\kappa t))}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>X</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>X</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>U</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#954;<!-- &#954; width:49.687ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle Y(t)=Y(0)+2A\\left(X(0)+{\\frac {V(0)}{2B}}\\right)t-{\\frac {\\Omega _{0}}{B\\kappa }}V(0)\\sin(\\kappa t)+{\\frac {2\\Omega _{0}}{\\kappa ^{2}}}U(0)(1-\\cos(\\kappa t))}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>Y</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>Y</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow>\n<mi>X</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>V</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>B</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mi>B</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{0}=A-B}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\Omega _{0}=A-B}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2ead3dadeffbf8dcc79909aa6dc10d59cf163e2b" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:12.178ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is the angular velocity of galactic rotation for the local standard of rest, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\kappa ={\\sqrt {-4\\Omega _{0}B}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#954;<!-- &#954; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>4</mn>\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#937;<!-- &#937; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mi>B</mi>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\kappa ={\\sqrt {-4\\Omega _{0}B}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\kappa ={\\sqrt {-4\\Omega _{0}B}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6dda165f42b8a36c5cc943bd35fe1549e0fff3e1" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:14.228ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span> is the "epicyclic frequency", and &#957; is the vertical oscillation frequency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[141]</a></sup> For the sun, the present values of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">U</span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">V</span>, and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">W</span> are estimated as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle (U(0),V(0),W(0))=(10.00,5.25,7.17)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>U</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mi>V</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mi>W</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>10.00</mn>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mn>5.25</mn>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mn>7.17</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle (U(0),V(0),W(0))=(10.00,5.25,7.17)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle (U(0),V(0),W(0))=(10.00,5.25,7.17)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e0a0b978fb1b35cf27184ca919054d0dbf09e4bb" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; We take <span class="texhtml"><i>X</i>(0)</span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>Y</i>(0)</span> to be zero and <span class="texhtml"><i>Z</i>(0)</span> is estimated to be 17 parsecs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup> This model implies that the Sun circulates around a point that is itself going around the galaxy. The period of the Sun\'s circulation around the point is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle 2\\pi /\\kappa }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>&#960;<!-- &#960; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#954;<!-- &#954; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 2\\pi /\\kappa }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle 2\\pi /\\kappa }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f16634dc7b3748efd1772f5c2060f227f942b98c" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.996ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>. which, using the equivalence that a parsec equals 1&#160;km/s times 0.978 million years, comes to 166 million years, shorter than the time it takes for the point to go around the galaxy. In the (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">X, Y</span>) coordinates, the Sun describes an ellipse around the point, whose length in the <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">Y</span> direction is\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle 2\\times {\\sqrt {\\left({\\frac {2\\Omega _{0}}{\\kappa ^{2}}}U(0)\\right)^{2}+\\left({\\frac {\\Omega _{0}}{B\\kappa }}V(0)\\right)^{2}}}=1035{\\text{ parsec}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mo>&#215;<!-- &#215; width:28.518ex; height:5.676ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The oscillation in the <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">Z</span> direction takes the Sun\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt {\\left({\\frac {W(0)}{\\nu }}\\right)^{2}+Z(0)^{2}}}=98{\\text{ parsec}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msqrt>\n<msup>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi>W</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>Z</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>0</mn>\n<msup>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</msqrt>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>98</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>&#160;parsec</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt {\\left({\\frac {W(0)}{\\nu }}\\right)^{2}+Z(0)^{2}}}=98{\\text{ parsec}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\sqrt {\\left({\\frac {W(0)}{\\nu }}\\right)^{2}+Z(0)^{2}}}=98{\\text{ parsec}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/af1e32894493249abd7f1ea70098c48f3427c100" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:33.941ex; height:7.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>above the galactic plane and the same distance below it, with a period of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle 2\\pi /\\nu }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>&#960;<!-- &#960; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mi>&#957;<!-- &#957; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 2\\pi /\\nu }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle 2\\pi /\\nu }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f59f413c4a65b8a9e8e41e2093fb7ff3c7414dfa" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.889ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> or 83 million years, approximately 2.7 times per orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[143]</a></sup>  Although <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle 2\\pi /\\Omega _{0}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>&#960;<!-- &#960; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#937;<!-- &#937; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle 2\\pi /\\Omega _{0}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle 2\\pi /\\Omega _{0}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/929b22f32322e47f6120955cf51e50f4d23d9b79" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:6.389ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is 222 million years, the value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Omega }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#937;<!-- &#937; --></mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Omega }</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\Omega " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/24b0d5ca6f381068d756f6337c08e0af9d1eeb6f" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.678ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> at the point around which the Sun circulates is\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\Omega \\approx \\Omega _{0}-{\\frac {2A}{R_{0}}}\\Delta X\\approx 26.1{\\text{ km/s/kpc}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#937;<!-- &#937; --></mi>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#937;<!-- &#937; --></mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>A</mi>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>X</mi>\n<mo>&#8776;<!-- &#8776; --></mo>\n<mn>26.1</mn>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtext>&#160;km/s/kpc</mtext>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\Omega \\approx \\Omega _{0}-{\\frac {2A}{R_{0}}}\\Delta X\\approx 26.1{\\text{ km/s/kpc}}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\Omega \\approx \\Omega _{0}-{\\frac {2A}{R_{0}}}\\Delta X\\approx 26.1{\\text{ km/s/kpc}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/51a9ec31c4bb7b7ea53d7ff6ba0a5c4d5c1c7caa" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:35.777ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>(see <a href="/wiki/Oort_constants" title="Oort constants">Oort constants</a>), corresponding to 235 million years, and this is the time that the point takes to go once around the galaxy. Other stars with the same value of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle X+V/(2B)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>X</mi>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>V</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mn>2</mn>\n<mi>B</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle X+V/(2B)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle X+V/(2B)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/74a9617775983bbc10039de9637300dea9c2c425" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:12.506ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> have take the same amount of time to go around the galaxy as the sun and thus remain in the same general vicinity as the Sun.\n</p><p>The Sun\'s orbit around the Milky Way is perturbed due to the non-uniform mass distribution in Milky Way, such as that in and between the galactic spiral arms. It has been argued that the Sun\'s passage through the higher density spiral arms often coincides with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinctions</a> on Earth, perhaps due to increased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impact_events" title="Impact events">impact events</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extinction_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-extinction-150">[144]</a></sup> It takes the Solar System about 225&#8211;250 million years to complete one orbit through the Milky Way (a <i><a href="/wiki/Galactic_year" title="Galactic year">galactic year</a></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fn10_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-fn10-151">[145]</a></sup> so it is thought to have completed 20&#8211;25 orbits during the lifetime of the Sun. The <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">orbital speed</a> of the Solar System about the center of the Milky Way is approximately 251&#160;km/s (156&#160;mi/s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.newscientist.com_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-space.newscientist.com-152">[146]</a></sup> At this speed, it takes around 1,190 years for the Solar System to travel a distance of 1 light-year, or 7 days to travel 1 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[147]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Milky Way is moving with respect to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background_radiation" title="Cosmic microwave background radiation">cosmic microwave background radiation</a> (CMB) in the direction of the constellation <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(constellation)" title="Hydra (constellation)">Hydra</a> with a speed of 550&#160;km/s, and the Sun\'s resultant velocity with respect to the CMB is about 370&#160;km/s in the direction of <a href="/wiki/Crater_(constellation)" title="Crater (constellation)">Crater</a> or <a href="/wiki/Leo_(constellation)" title="Leo (constellation)">Leo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[148]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theoretical_problems">Theoretical problems</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_full_sun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Map_of_the_full_sun.jpg/220px-Map_of_the_full_sun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Map_of_the_full_sun.jpg/330px-Map_of_the_full_sun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Map_of_the_full_sun.jpg/440px-Map_of_the_full_sun.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_full_sun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the full Sun by STEREO and <a href="/wiki/Solar_Dynamics_Observatory" title="Solar Dynamics Observatory">SDO</a> spacecraft</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coronal_heating_problem">Coronal heating problem</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Corona" title="Corona">Corona</a></div>\n<p>The temperature of the photosphere is approximately 6,000&#160;K, whereas the temperature of the corona reaches 1,000,000&#8211;2,000,000&#160;K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erdelyi2007_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-93">[88]</a></sup> The high temperature of the corona shows that it is heated by something other than direct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_conduction" title="Heat conduction">heat conduction</a> from the photosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell2001_96-6"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2001-96">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is thought that the energy necessary to heat the corona is provided by turbulent motion in the convection zone below the photosphere, and two main mechanisms have been proposed to explain coronal heating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erdelyi2007_93-4"><a href="#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-93">[88]</a></sup> The first is <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">wave</a> heating, in which sound, gravitational or magnetohydrodynamic waves are produced by turbulence in the convection zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erdelyi2007_93-5"><a href="#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-93">[88]</a></sup> These waves travel upward and dissipate in the corona, depositing their energy in the ambient matter in the form of heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alfven_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alfven-155">[149]</a></sup> The other is <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic</a> heating, in which magnetic energy is continuously built up by photospheric motion and released through <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_reconnection" title="Magnetic reconnection">magnetic reconnection</a> in the form of large <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flares</a> and myriad similar but smaller events&#8212;<a href="/wiki/Nanoflares" title="Nanoflares">nanoflares</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parker2_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parker2-156">[150]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Currently, it is unclear whether waves are an efficient heating mechanism. All waves except <a href="/wiki/Alfv%C3%A9n_wave" title="Alfv&#233;n wave">Alfv&#233;n waves</a> have been found to dissipate or refract before reaching the corona.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sturrock_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sturrock-157">[151]</a></sup> In addition, Alfv&#233;n waves do not easily dissipate in the corona. Current research focus has therefore shifted towards flare heating mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Erdelyi2007_93-6"><a href="#cite_note-Erdelyi2007-93">[88]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Faint_young_Sun_problem">Faint young Sun problem</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Faint_young_Sun_paradox" title="Faint young Sun paradox">Faint young Sun paradox</a></div>\n<p>Theoretical models of the Sun\'s development suggest that 3.8 to 2.5 billion years ago, during the <a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean">Archean eon</a>, the Sun was only about 75% as bright as it is today. Such a weak star would not have been able to sustain liquid water on Earth\'s surface, and thus life should not have been able to develop. However, the geological record demonstrates that Earth has remained at a fairly constant temperature throughout its history, and that the young Earth was somewhat warmer than it is today. One theory among scientists is that the atmosphere of the young Earth contained much larger quantities of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>) than are present today, which trapped enough heat to compensate for the smaller amount of <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a> reaching it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kasting_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kasting-158">[152]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, examination of Archaean sediments appears inconsistent with the hypothesis of high greenhouse concentrations. Instead, the moderate temperature range may be explained by a lower surface <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> brought about by less continental area and the "lack of biologically induced cloud condensation nuclei". This would have led to increased absorption of solar energy, thereby compensating for the lower solar output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosing_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosing-159">[153]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observational_history">Observational history</span></h2>\n<p>The enormous effect of the Sun on Earth has been recognized since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_times" title="Prehistoric times">prehistoric times</a>, and the Sun has been <a href="/wiki/Solar_deity" title="Solar deity">regarded by some cultures</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_understanding">Early understanding</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1438" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg/220px-Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg/330px-Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg/440px-Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solvognen_DO-6865_2000.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Trundholm_sun_chariot" title="Trundholm sun chariot">Trundholm sun chariot</a> pulled by a horse is a sculpture believed to be illustrating an important part of <a href="/wiki/Nordic_Bronze_Age" title="Nordic Bronze Age">Nordic Bronze Age</a> mythology. The sculpture is probably from around 1350 <a href="/wiki/Anno_Domini" title="Anno Domini">BC</a>. It is displayed at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Denmark" title="National Museum of Denmark">National Museum of Denmark</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/The_Sun_in_culture" title="The Sun in culture">The Sun in culture</a></div>\n<p>The Sun has been an object of veneration in many cultures throughout human history. Humanity\'s most fundamental understanding of the Sun is as the luminous disk in the <a href="/wiki/Sky" title="Sky">sky</a>, whose presence above the <a href="/wiki/Horizon" title="Horizon">horizon</a> creates day and whose absence causes night. In many prehistoric and ancient cultures, the Sun was thought to be a <a href="/wiki/Solar_deity" title="Solar deity">solar deity</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> entity. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun_worship" title="Sun worship">Worship of the Sun</a> was central to civilizations such as the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egyptians</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca" title="Inca">Inca</a> of South America and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">Aztecs</a> of what is now <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. In religions such as <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, the Sun is still considered a god. Many ancient monuments were constructed with solar phenomena in mind; for example, stone <a href="/wiki/Megalith" title="Megalith">megaliths</a> accurately mark the summer or winter <a href="/wiki/Solstice" title="Solstice">solstice</a> (some of the most prominent megaliths are located in <a href="/wiki/Nabta_Playa" title="Nabta Playa">Nabta Playa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>; <a href="/wiki/Mnajdra" title="Mnajdra">Mnajdra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> and at <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>); <a href="/wiki/Newgrange" title="Newgrange">Newgrange</a>, a prehistoric human-built mount in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, was designed to detect the winter solstice; the pyramid of <a href="/wiki/El_Castillo,_Chichen_Itza" title="El Castillo, Chichen Itza">El Castillo</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chich%C3%A9n_Itz%C3%A1" title="Chich&#233;n Itz&#225;">Chich&#233;n Itz&#225;</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> is designed to cast shadows in the shape of serpents climbing the <a href="/wiki/Pyramid" title="Pyramid">pyramid</a> at the vernal and autumnal <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">equinoxes</a>.\n</p><p>The Egyptians portrayed the god <a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a> as being carried across the sky in a solar barque, accompanied by lesser gods, and to the Greeks, he was <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a>, carried by a chariot drawn by fiery horses. From the reign of <a href="/wiki/Elagabalus" title="Elagabalus">Elagabalus</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">late Roman Empire</a> the Sun\'s birthday was a holiday celebrated as <a href="/wiki/Sol_Invictus" title="Sol Invictus">Sol Invictus</a> (literally "Unconquered Sun") soon after the winter solstice, which may have been an antecedent to Christmas. Regarding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fixed_star" title="Fixed star">fixed stars</a>, the Sun appears from Earth to revolve once a year along the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">zodiac</a>, and so Greek astronomers categorized it as one of the seven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_planets" title="Classical planets">planets</a> (Greek <i>planetes</i>, "wanderer"); the naming of the <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_days_of_the_week" title="Names of the days of the week">days of the weeks</a> after the seven planets dates to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman era</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed-160">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-almagest_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-almagest-161">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[156]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_scientific_understanding">Development of scientific understanding</span></h3>\n<p>In the early first millennium BC, <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomy" title="Babylonian astronomy">Babylonian astronomers</a> observed that the Sun\'s motion along the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> is not uniform, though they did not know why; it is today known that this is due to the movement of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> in an <a href="/wiki/Elliptic_orbit" title="Elliptic orbit">elliptic orbit</a> around the Sun, with Earth moving faster when it is nearer to the Sun at <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion</a> and moving slower when it is farther away at <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">aphelion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[157]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the first people to offer a scientific or philosophical explanation for the Sun was the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras</a>. He reasoned that it was not the <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariot</a> of <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a>, but instead a giant flaming ball of metal even larger than the land of the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnesus</a> and that the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> reflected the light of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[158]</a></sup> For teaching this <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>, he was imprisoned by the authorities and <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">sentenced to death</a>, though he was later released through the intervention of <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">Pericles</a>. <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> estimated the distance between Earth and the Sun in the 3rd century BC as "of stadia <a href="/wiki/Myriad" title="Myriad">myriads</a> 400 and 80000", the translation of which is ambiguous, implying either 4,080,000 <a href="/wiki/Stadion_(unit)" title="Stadion (unit)">stadia</a> (755,000&#160;km) or 804,000,000 stadia (148 to 153 million kilometers or 0.99 to 1.02 AU); the latter value is correct to within a few percent. In the 1st century AD, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> estimated the distance as 1,210 times <a href="/wiki/Earth_radius" title="Earth radius">the radius of Earth</a>, approximately 7.71&#160;million kilometers (0.0515&#160;AU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[159]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The theory that the Sun is the center around which the planets orbit was first proposed by the ancient Greek <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> in the 3rd century BC, and later adopted by <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_of_Seleucia" title="Seleucus of Seleucia">Seleucus of Seleucia</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">Heliocentrism</a>). This view was developed in a more detailed <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_model" title="Mathematical model">mathematical model</a> of a heliocentric system in the 16th century by <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a>.\n</p><p>Observations of sunspots were recorded during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> (206 BC&#8211;AD 220) by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">Chinese astronomers</a>, who maintained records of these observations for centuries. <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> also provided a description of sunspots in the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[160]</a></sup> The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a> in the early 17th century permitted detailed observations of <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspots</a> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Harriot" title="Thomas Harriot">Thomas Harriot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> and other astronomers. Galileo posited that sunspots were on the surface of the Sun rather than small objects passing between Earth and the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[161]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomy_in_medieval_Islam" title="Astronomy in medieval Islam">Arabic astronomical contributions</a> include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_J%C4%81bir_al-Harr%C4%81n%C4%AB_al-Batt%C4%81n%C4%AB" title="Muhammad ibn J&#257;bir al-Harr&#257;n&#299; al-Batt&#257;n&#299;">Albatenius</a>\' discovery that the direction of the Sun\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apogee" title="Apogee">apogee</a> (the place in the Sun\'s orbit against the fixed stars where it seems to be moving slowest) is changing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[162]</a></sup> (In modern heliocentric terms, this is caused by a gradual motion of the aphelion of the <i>Earth\'s</i> orbit). <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Yunus" title="Ibn Yunus">Ibn Yunus</a> observed more than 10,000 entries for the Sun\'s position for many years using a large <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">astrolabe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[163]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sun-bonatti.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="401" data-file-width="563" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Sun-bonatti.png/220px-Sun-bonatti.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Sun-bonatti.png/330px-Sun-bonatti.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Sun-bonatti.png/440px-Sun-bonatti.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sun-bonatti.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sol, the Sun, from a 1550 edition of <a href="/wiki/Guido_Bonatti" title="Guido Bonatti">Guido Bonatti</a>\'s <i>Liber astronomiae</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>From an observation of a <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">transit of Venus</a> in 1032, the Persian astronomer and polymath <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a> concluded that Venus is closer to Earth than the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldstein_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein-170">[164]</a></sup> In 1672 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giovanni_Cassini" title="Giovanni Cassini">Giovanni Cassini</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Richer" title="Jean Richer">Jean Richer</a> determined the distance to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and were thereby able to calculate the distance to the Sun.\n</p><p>In 1666, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> observed the Sun\'s light using a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prism_(optics)" title="Prism (optics)">prism</a>, and showed that it is made up of light of many colors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[165]</a></sup> In 1800, <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> radiation beyond the red part of the solar spectrum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[166]</a></sup> The 19th century saw advancement in spectroscopic studies of the Sun; <a href="/wiki/Joseph_von_Fraunhofer" title="Joseph von Fraunhofer">Joseph von Fraunhofer</a> recorded more than 600 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_lines" title="Absorption lines">absorption lines</a> in the spectrum, the strongest of which are still often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Fraunhofer_lines" title="Fraunhofer lines">Fraunhofer lines</a>. In the early years of the modern scientific era, the source of the Sun\'s energy was a significant puzzle. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_Kelvin" title="Lord Kelvin">Lord Kelvin</a> suggested that the Sun is a gradually cooling liquid body that is radiating an internal store of heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kelvin_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-kelvin-173">[167]</a></sup> Kelvin and <a href="/wiki/Hermann_von_Helmholtz" title="Hermann von Helmholtz">Hermann von Helmholtz</a> then proposed a <a href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Helmholtz_mechanism" title="Kelvin&#8211;Helmholtz mechanism">gravitational contraction</a> mechanism to explain the energy output, but the resulting age estimate was only 20 million years, well short of the time span of at least 300 million years suggested by some geological discoveries of that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kelvin_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-kelvin-173">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[168]</a></sup> In 1890 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Norman_Lockyer" title="Joseph Norman Lockyer">Joseph Lockyer</a>, who discovered helium in the solar spectrum, proposed a meteoritic hypothesis for the formation and evolution of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[169]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Not until 1904 was a documented solution offered. <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> suggested that the Sun\'s output could be maintained by an internal source of heat, and suggested <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> as the source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[170]</a></sup> However, it would be <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> who would provide the essential clue to the source of the Sun\'s energy output with his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass-energy_equivalence" title="Mass-energy equivalence">mass-energy equivalence</a> relation <span class="nowrap"><i>E</i> = <i>mc</i><sup>2</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[171]</a></sup> In 1920, Sir <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Arthur Eddington</a> proposed that the pressures and temperatures at the core of the Sun could produce a nuclear fusion reaction that merged hydrogen (protons) into helium nuclei, resulting in a production of energy from the net change in mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[172]</a></sup> The preponderance of hydrogen in the Sun was confirmed in 1925 by <a href="/wiki/Cecilia_Payne-Gaposchkin" title="Cecilia Payne-Gaposchkin">Cecilia Payne</a> using the <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionization</a> theory developed by <a href="/wiki/Meghnad_Saha" title="Meghnad Saha">Meghnad Saha</a>, an Indian physicist. The theoretical concept of fusion was developed in the 1930s by the astrophysicists <a href="/wiki/Subrahmanyan_Chandrasekhar" title="Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar">Subrahmanyan Chandrasekhar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_Bethe" title="Hans Bethe">Hans Bethe</a>. Hans Bethe calculated the details of the two main energy-producing nuclear reactions that power the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bethe_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bethe-179">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bethe2_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bethe2-180">[174]</a></sup> In 1957, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Burbidge" title="Margaret Burbidge">Margaret Burbidge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Burbidge" title="Geoffrey Burbidge">Geoffrey Burbidge</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Alfred_Fowler" title="William Alfred Fowler">William Fowler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Hoyle" title="Fred Hoyle">Fred Hoyle</a> showed that most of the elements in the universe have been <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">synthesized</a> by nuclear reactions inside stars, some like the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[175]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_space_missions">Solar space missions</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Solar_observatory" title="Solar observatory">Solar observatory</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunspots_and_Solar_Flares.jpg"><img alt="" on 1:29 pm, EST, 13 March 2012</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" alt="File:Moon transit of sun large.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Moon_transit_of_sun_large.ogv/220px--Moon_transit_of_sun_large.ogv.jpg"
